- [ ] **M205** — Other "preflight-to-action" re-check gaps (spawned from M204). The M204 pattern — check → time passes → act — may repeat elsewhere:
      - **RAM preflight:** same shape. User could close other apps + free RAM between preflight and start (benign), OR open a RAM-hungry app that puts convert at OOM risk (adversarial). Need similar re-check.
      - **`outputUsable` check:** Step 3 verifies output dir parent is writable. But the dir could be unmounted (external drive), converted to read-only (filesystem flip), or deleted (user rearranged folders). Re-check at start().
      - **`sourceReadable` check:** same — source could be unmounted between preflight and start.

- [x] **M199 (extend rate-limit dependency to /retry + /admin/purge + /recommend)** — Iter-136 closed the 8-iter rate-limit backlog. iter-124 M187 added `check_rate_limit` to POST /jobs and POST /estimate — explicitly left /retry, /admin/purge, and other endpoints for "later". Eight iters later, "later" is now.
      **Scope additions:**
      - `POST /jobs/{job_id}/retry` — real DoS vector. Retry spawns the full convert pipeline (subprocess + HF download + compute work). An unprotected client could fire `/retry` in a loop to pin CPU + saturate disk I/O.
      - `POST /admin/purge` — DB sweep + row delete work. Less severe than /retry but still cheap to extend; no reason to leave one admin endpoint unprotected when the rate limiter is already wired.
      - `GET /recommend/{model_id:path}` — hits HF `api.model_info` on every call. Same exhaustion vector as `/estimate`. An unprotected attacker could flood `/recommend` to burn through the server's shared HF rate budget, indirectly DoS-ing all HF-dependent endpoints for legit users.
      **What's intentionally LEFT OUT:**
      - `DELETE /jobs/{job_id}` (cancel) — just sets a threading.Event + DB row update. Negligible work; rate-limit noise > real protection.
      - `GET /jobs`, `GET /jobs/{id}`, `GET /jobs/{id}/logs`, `GET /queue` — DB-only reads. M187's rationale ("keep liveness probes cheap") applies: rate-limiting lookup endpoints adds operator friction for minimal DoS gain.
      - `GET /jobs/{id}/stream` — already rate-limited by M188 iter-125 via separate `SSE_MAX_PER_IP` + `SSE_MAX_GLOBAL` caps (different semantic: stream open rate vs. concurrent connections).
      **Fix (iter 136):** added `Depends(check_rate_limit)` to the 3 endpoint decorators (ordered BEFORE `Depends(check_auth)` — rate-limit applies even to failed-auth attempts, defends against auth-brute-force flooding, matching M187's original design).
      **Tests:** extended `jang-server/tests/test_rate_limit.py::test_rate_limit_applied_to_high_cost_endpoints` to include the 3 new endpoints. Renamed the failure-message prefix from `M187` to `M199` for the new cases so regressions are clearly attributable to the iter-136 extension, not the iter-124 baseline.
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:1084` (retry), `:1311` (admin/purge), `:1275` (recommend). 69/69 jang-server tests pass (test count unchanged — one existing test body extended).
      **Meta-lesson — finish small easy items before they accumulate into a pile of "later".** iter-124 deferred `/retry` + `/admin/purge` because the focus was `/jobs` + `/estimate`. Eight iters later, the deferral was still sitting in the forecast bullets. **Rule: if a small easy extension of the current iter's work is deferred, rank it in the forecast. If it stays deferred past 3 iters, promote it — deferrals past 3 iters usually mean the TOP priority isn't actually top, not that the small item is hard.** Parallels iter-127 M190's 4-bump rule on a different axis (defer count, not allowlist bumps).
      **Meta-lesson — attribute regressions to the iter that introduced the fix, not the earliest related one.** When extending iter-124's test, I renamed the failure-message prefix from `M187` to `M199` for the NEW entries. If someone regresses `/retry`'s rate-limit in iter-180, the message reads `M199 regression`, pointing at the iter-136 commit that added `/retry` coverage (not the iter-124 baseline). **Rule: when extending an existing invariant to cover new cases, attribute the NEW coverage to its introducing iter. Makes git-blame precise when hunting regressions.**
      **Meta-lesson — explicitly document what's OUT of scope + why.** The audit entry's "intentionally left out" section prevents a future iter from "fixing" the missing rate-limit on DELETE /jobs (cancel). Without the explanation, a thorough auditor might add `Depends(check_rate_limit)` to DELETE thinking they're closing a gap, when the rationale is "rate-limit noise > real protection here". **Rule: when making a scope decision that EXCLUDES something a future auditor might add, document the exclusion + rationale in the AUDIT_CHECKLIST entry. Sin by commission is easier to diagnose than sin by omission.**
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M198 (retroactive invariants for JANGQuantizer.swiftpm — M185 URL injection + M186 silent swallow)** — Iter-135 applies iter-134 M197's meta-rule RETROACTIVELY: every security fix needs a paired invariant. Audit across iters 111-197 found M185 (iter 120) and M186 (iter 121) both fixed real bugs in the JANGQuantizer.swiftpm frontend but neither had a paired regression test. The package has NO Tests/ directory today.
      **Audit results (iters 111-197):** confirmed every other security fix has an invariant:
      - M177 (except: pass) → `test_exception_handling_invariant.py`
      - M178 (SSRF webhook) → `test_webhook_ssrf.py`
      - M179 (auth on GETs) → `test_auth_enforcement.py`
      - M180 (SSE subscriber leak) → `test_sse_subscribers_cleanup.py`
      - M181 (hardcoded HF token) → `test_no_hardcoded_secrets.py`
      - M182 (repo-wide secrets) → `test_no_hardcoded_secrets_repo_wide.py`
      - M183 (non-source files) → `test_no_hardcoded_secrets_repo_wide.py`
      - M184 (SKIP_DIR gap) → `test_no_hardcoded_secrets_repo_wide.py`
      - M185 (URL injection) → **MISSING**
      - M186 (silent swallow) → **MISSING**
      - M187-M197 → all have paired invariants
      Two gaps, both in JANGQuantizer.swiftpm. Rather than stand up a SwiftPM test target just for these two, used source-inspection invariants in Python (same pattern as M197's cross-language parity test — Python tests can read Swift source).
      **Fix (iter 135):** new `ralph_runner/tests/test_frontend_quantizer_invariants.py` with 5 tests:
      - `test_list_jobs_uses_url_components_not_interpolation` (M185 APIClient): pins `URLComponents` + `URLQueryItem` are used in `listJobs`; negative-pin that `"/jobs?...\\("` string-interpolation bug can't return.
      - `test_queue_view_cancel_retry_use_do_catch_not_try_optional` (M186 JobCard): strips comments (the M186 docstring MENTIONS `try? await` as the pre-fix bug) and asserts no `try? await` in live code.
      - `test_queue_view_has_actionError_state_for_error_surface` (M186): pins `@State private var actionError` + its `Text(err)` rendering. Captures-without-rendering would be an invisible security property.
      - `test_settings_check_connection_uses_do_catch_with_last_error` (M185 SettingsView): pins the "Check Connection" button uses do/catch + lastError.
      - `test_settings_has_last_error_rendering` (M185): pins `if let err = lastError` rendering.
      **Technique — comment-stripping before the `try? await` check.** The M186 docstring comment legitimately quotes the bug pattern (`Task { try? await api.X(...) }`) to explain what it fixed. A naive "no `try? await` anywhere" check would false-positive on that comment. The test strips `//`/`///` lines before searching — catches live-code reintroduction without flagging the educational comment. Inverse of iter-130 M193's `rfind` lesson (both are "test must distinguish rationale comments from live code").
      **Why invariants-on-render matter, not just invariants-on-capture:** a future edit could add `catch { lastError = "..." }` but remove the `Text(err)` rendering. Capture without render = invisible error = same UX as the pre-fix silent swallow. The test pins BOTH sides.
      **Evidence:** `ralph_runner/tests/test_frontend_quantizer_invariants.py` (new, ~140 lines). 12/12 collectible ralph_runner invariants pass. 69/69 jang-server tests pass.
      **Meta-lesson — audit INVARIANT coverage as a first-class property.** M197 made the rule "every security fix needs a paired invariant"; M198 applied the rule BACKWARDS by auditing the last 20 iters of security fixes. Found exactly 2 gaps out of ~20 items — ~90% coverage already, but the 10% gap covered real bugs that could silently reopen. **Rule: periodically audit INVARIANT coverage alongside bug-coverage. The audit doesn't need to be exhaustive every iter, but after ~10-20 security fixes accumulate, spend one iter doing a full invariant-coverage sweep.** Same philosophy as "periodically run the full test suite even if CI runs a subset" — detect silent gaps before they matter.
      **Meta-lesson — Python tests CAN invariantize Swift code without a Swift test target.** Setting up a new Swift test target adds scaffolding (Package.swift edits, test-only deps, Xcode project ref updates) that's unwarranted for 2 invariants. The source-inspection approach from M197 works for any language — Python reads the Swift source as text + pins properties via regex/substring. **Rule: when adding invariants across language boundaries, prefer source-inspection from the EXISTING test framework over standing up a new test target.** Only stand up a native test target when you need runtime behavior (actual function calls, assertions on internal state) that source inspection can't replicate.
      **Meta-lesson — pin CAPTURE and RENDER, not just capture.** Error surfaces have two parts: "we caught the error" (state assignment) and "we showed it to the user" (view rendering). Either missing = same UX as silent swallow. Tests that only pin capture give false confidence. **Rule: for user-facing error properties, pin BOTH the state variable's existence AND its rendering in at least one Text/Label/Alert site.** Dual pins are ~2x the code; silent-swallow-via-missing-render is a subtle regression worth preventing.
      **Meta-lesson — comment stripping is a necessary preprocessing step for "no X in code" invariants.** When a rule is "live code must not contain pattern Y", the rationale comments explaining WHY usually MENTION Y. Naive substring checks false-positive on the comments. Strip `//` and `///` lines first, then search. **Rule: for "no bad pattern in code" invariants, strip single-line comments before the search. Block comments (`/* ... */`) also if used.** Trivial preprocessing, makes educational comments safe.
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M197 (mechanical cross-language parity invariant for redaction helpers)** — Iter-134 codifies iter-133 M196's meta-lesson. M196 fixed a parity DRIFT but didn't add a mechanism to PREVENT FUTURE drift. Without an invariant, the next iter to add a pattern on one side (Python or Swift) wouldn't know the OTHER side needs the matching pattern. M197 is the enforcement layer that makes parity an executable property.
      **Design:**
      - A new test file `ralph_runner/tests/test_redaction_cross_language_parity.py` with 3 tests.
      - `test_python_and_swift_cover_the_same_secret_shapes` — primary invariant. Extracts the pattern-declaration block from each file (between `_SECRET_REDACTIONS = [` / `def redact_for_log` for Python, and between `sensitivePatterns:` / `nonisolated static func scrubSensitive` for Swift), then checks each entry in the `SHAPE_TOKENS` taxonomy appears in BOTH blocks. Case-insensitive substring match so Python's regex-escaped `hooks\.slack\.com` and Swift's description `"Slack webhook secret"` both match on the keyword `slack`.
      - `test_python_pattern_block_has_at_least_seven_patterns` — coarse-count floor (`re.compile(` occurrences ≥ 6) so a refactor that drops most patterns doesn't silently pass the taxonomy test.
      - `test_swift_pattern_block_has_at_least_seven_patterns` — parallel floor (`#"` open delimiters ≥ 6).
      **Taxonomy (7 shapes):** HF `hf_<20+>`, HF legacy `huggingface_<20+>`, Bearer tokens (both `Bearer` and `bearer` case variants), OpenAI `sk-<20+>` / `sk-proj-<20+>`, Slack webhook (keyword `slack`), Discord webhook (keyword `discord`), URL query secrets (keyword `api_key`). Each shape has a list of alternative identifiers so Python's regex syntax + Swift's regex syntax + Swift's description strings can all satisfy the coverage assertion.
      **Key design decisions:**
      - **Keyword-in-block, not regex-equality.** Python `re` and Swift `NSRegularExpression` have different syntax (backslash-escape rules, anchors, lookbehind support). Comparing regex strings literally would fire on every trivial syntax difference. Comparing keyword presence is resilient to syntax variations and catches the DRIFT class that matters (missing shape, not subtle regex difference).
      - **Case-insensitive block comparison.** Python regex has `\bBearer`, Swift has `(?i)\bbearer`. Description strings have mixed case (`Slack webhook secret` vs `hooks\.slack\.com`). Lowercasing the block before token-search sidesteps the case mismatch.
      - **Block scoping via start/end markers.** Without scoping, the test would false-positive on pattern names mentioned in unrelated comments elsewhere in the file. Extracting the declaration region narrows the search.
      - **Per-pattern keyword alternatives.** Each SHAPE_TOKENS entry has a list of keywords (`["hooks.slack.com", "slack.com"]` originally, trimmed to just `"slack"`). Any ONE match counts as coverage — the test is tolerant of pattern implementation differences while still requiring presence.
      **Allowlist maintenance (unrelated bundled fix):** the M182/M183 repo-wide secrets sweep was flagging test fixtures introduced in iter-130/131/132/133 (redaction unit tests + DB backfill fixtures + Swift OpenAI parity tests). Added 5 new allowlist entries with rationale. This follows the pattern M181's initial fix: allowlist clearly-fake test fixtures, rotate real secrets.
      **Future upgrades** (documented in the test's module docstring):
      - Switch to AST-based parse of the Python tuple list + Swift regex-array tokenization for structural comparison (vs keyword-in-source).
      - Single source-of-truth: extract patterns into `sensitive-patterns.yaml` that both sides compile from at build time. M197 would become a no-op guarding the shared file.
      **Evidence:** `ralph_runner/tests/test_redaction_cross_language_parity.py` (new file, 197 lines). `ralph_runner/tests/test_no_hardcoded_secrets_repo_wide.py` allowlist updated.
      **Tests:** 3 new parity tests + 5 new allowlist entries for the M181/M182/M183 repo-wide sweep. 69/69 jang-server tests still pass; 7/7 collectible ralph_runner invariant tests pass.
      **Meta-lesson — a security FIX without an INVARIANT is half the work.** M196 fixed the drift that had accumulated over 119 iters. Without M197, the next iter that adds a new secret shape on only one side would silently re-open the drift. The invariant is what turns "we fixed it" into "it stays fixed." **Rule: every substantive security fix needs a paired invariant that asserts the fixed state. A fix without an invariant is a time-limited solution with a nondeterministic expiration.** Parallels iter-114 M179's test-driven audit pattern.
      **Meta-lesson — extract-and-test-region > test-the-whole-file for multi-purpose files.** Both Python `server.py` and Swift `DiagnosticsBundle.swift` contain comments, code, tests, and many other sections. A "does `slack` appear in the file?" test would false-positive on unrelated mentions. Narrowing to a region (pattern-declaration block) makes the test both precise AND maintainable. **Rule: when testing a property of a specific code region, anchor the test to that region via start/end markers. Don't scan the whole file unless the property applies to the whole file.** Parallels iter-127 M190's structural-slicing lesson at a different scale (function body vs. declaration block).
      **Meta-lesson — taxonomy lists belong in the test file, not the source.** I put `SHAPE_TOKENS` in the test file rather than in `server.py` or `DiagnosticsBundle.swift`. Reasoning: the taxonomy is a TEST-time concept ("these are the shapes we claim to cover"). The source-level truth is the pattern lists themselves. Putting the taxonomy in source would create a third place-of-truth that could drift from the other two. **Rule: when an invariant compares two things, the description of WHAT'S being compared belongs with the INVARIANT, not with the things being compared.** The test IS the taxonomy contract; the sources are the implementations.
      **Meta-lesson — keyword-in-source is the pragmatic parity check; structural parse is the robust one.** The test uses substring-in-block which catches shape-level drift but would miss a pattern that LOOKS like coverage but doesn't actually MATCH anything useful (e.g., `hf_something_else_entirely` matching the `hf_` keyword but targeting a different shape). A stronger check would parse the tuple lists on both sides structurally. Current approach is 80% value / 20% effort; structural parse is 99% / 80% effort. **Rule: for drift-prevention invariants, start with pragmatic substring-based checks and upgrade to structural parsers only when you observe real false-negatives or the maintenance burden of manual review drops below the structural-parser implementation cost.** Same philosophy as "prefer simple tests that catch 80% of regressions over complex tests that catch 100%."
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M196 (JANGStudio DiagnosticsBundle.scrubSensitive — cross-language parity with jang-server redact_for_log)** — Iter-133 closed the cross-process/cross-language dimension of credential hygiene. The JANG stack has two independent redaction helpers — Python's `redact_for_log` (jang-server, iter-130 M193) and Swift's `DiagnosticsBundle.scrubSensitive` (JANGStudio, iter-14 M22e). Before M196 they had drifted apart: Python had 5 patterns, Swift had 4, with only 2 in common (HF + Bearer). A user whose diagnostics bundle inherited secrets via any of the 3 missing shapes would ship them in a bug report despite the Python side being clean.
      **Parity audit (before M196):**
      | Pattern | Python (redact_for_log) | Swift (scrubSensitive) |
      |---------|:-----------------------:|:----------------------:|
      | `hf_<20+>` | ✅ | ✅ |
      | `huggingface_<20+>` legacy | ❌ (in M182 sweep, not in redact_for_log) | ✅ |
      | `Authorization: Bearer …` | ✅ | ✅ (separate pattern, stricter) |
      | `Bearer <16+>` generic | ✅ | ✅ (20+ — stricter than Python's 16+) |
      | `sk-<20+>` / `sk-proj-<20+>` | ✅ | ❌ |
      | Slack webhook secret | ✅ (host kept) | ❌ |
      | Discord webhook secret | ✅ (host kept) | ❌ |
      | URL query `?api_key=`/`?token=`/`?access_token=`/`?auth=` | ✅ (param kept) | ❌ |
      **Fixes applied (both sides):**
      - **Swift**: added 3 patterns (OpenAI keys, Slack webhook secret path, Discord webhook secret path, URL query secrets). Changed the `sensitivePatterns` tuple shape from `(description, pattern)` to `(description, pattern, template)` so per-pattern templates like `"$1<redacted>"` allow partial redaction (keeping webhook host + query-param name for diagnostic context). Updated `scrubSensitive` to use the template field.
      - **Python**: added `huggingface_<20+>` legacy pattern to `_SECRET_REDACTIONS` (1 entry). Brings Python parity with Swift's legacy-HF coverage.
      **Design decision — partial replacement preserves diagnostic context.** Python's webhook pattern: `https://hooks.slack.com/***REDACTED***` (host kept). Swift's before M196: whole-match replacement would produce `<redacted>` (host lost). Fix: Swift now matches only the `/services/T0/B0/SECRET` path portion, so the scheme+host are preserved naturally. URL query: Swift uses template `$1<redacted>` with a capture group on `[?&]name=`, so `api_key=SECRET` becomes `api_key=<redacted>` (not `<redacted>`). Parallels Python's `m.group(1)` + REDACTED callable.
      **Tests (+5 Swift, +1 Python):**
      - Swift `DiagnosticsBundleTests`: `test_scrubs_openai_key` (sk- + sk-proj- variants), `test_scrubs_slack_webhook_but_keeps_host`, `test_scrubs_discord_webhook_but_keeps_host`, `test_scrubs_url_query_secret_keeps_param_name`, `test_scrubs_url_query_secret_multiple_param_names` (loops over api_key/token/access_token/auth), `test_scrub_preserves_non_secret_urls`. All pin PARTIAL replacement semantics + cross-language parity with Python's output shape.
      - Python `test_runtime_log_redaction.py`: `test_redact_for_log_huggingface_legacy_token` — pin the new legacy-HF pattern.
      **Evidence:** 21/21 Swift DiagnosticsBundleTests pass. 69/69 Python jang-server tests pass (was 68, +1).
      **Meta-lesson — cross-language parity needs an invariant to prevent drift.** Python and Swift helpers drifted for ~119 iters before anyone noticed. Neither side had a way to assert "we cover the same shapes as the other side." Each team added patterns for its own leak vectors without cross-checking. **Rule: when two independent implementations are supposed to enforce the SAME property, pin parity with a mechanical check — either a cross-language test that compares the pattern lists, or a single source-of-truth (YAML/JSON) both sides compile from.** The cheapest version: a checklist in a shared doc + a git-hook or CI step that asserts both files have the same pattern count + coverage descriptions.
      **Meta-lesson — partial-replacement > whole-match when diagnostic context matters.** Whole-match replacement is simpler but loses information an operator needs when triaging. A Slack webhook URL whose host is `hooks.slack.com` tells the operator WHICH service leaked; replacing the whole URL with `<redacted>` tells them only that there WAS a URL. Both are safe, but one is actionable and one isn't. **Rule: design redaction templates to preserve the non-secret scaffolding (URL schemes, hostnames, parameter names) that helps operators understand WHERE a secret was without revealing WHAT it was.** Parallels the iter-129 M192 principle of "document the why in the code" — diagnostic context is load-bearing for incident response.
      **Meta-lesson — refactoring data shape (2-tuple → 3-tuple) for forward compatibility is cheap when only one consumer exists.** Swift's `sensitivePatterns` was `[(String, String)]`; new partial-replacement patterns needed a per-pattern template. I could have kept the 2-tuple + computed templates by regex analysis, but that's magical. Instead changed to `[(description:String, pattern:String, template:String)]` with a default `"<redacted>"` for existing entries. Only one consumer (`scrubSensitive`) needed updating. Cheap refactor, clean API, no magic. **Rule: if a data shape is about to grow a third field and all consumers are in scope, refactor the shape explicitly rather than encoding the new info via conventions on the existing fields.** Same principle as iter-127 M190's structural-over-magical-anchor lesson.
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M195 (jang-server data-at-rest backfill for pre-M193/M194 persisted secrets)** — Iter-132 closed the time dimension of credential hygiene. M193 + M194 redact secrets at WRITE time, but rows persisted BEFORE those iters landed still sit on disk with unredacted tracebacks and phase_detail strings. An operator who rotated HF_UPLOAD_TOKEN after M181's leak STILL has the pre-rotation token embedded in old error tracebacks in WORK_DIR/jobs.db, readable by anyone with filesystem access to the server's work dir.
      **Scope audit of persisted fields:**
      - `_job_to_dict` serializes: `phase_detail`, `error`, plus structured sub-dicts (download, architecture, quantization, upload, result, timing). Of these only `phase_detail` + `error` are free-form strings that can carry exception text / URLs / tokens.
      - `log_lines` is NOT in `_job_to_dict` — in-memory-only, gone on restart. No backfill needed.
      - `webhook_url` is NOT in `_job_to_dict` — in-memory-only. No backfill needed (nice side-effect for secret hygiene: webhook URLs, which ARE secrets for Slack/Discord, don't survive a server restart).
      **Fix (iter 132):** added `_backfill_redact_persisted_logs()` that scans every row on startup, applies `redact_for_log` to `phase_detail` and `error` fields, writes back only rows that actually changed (avoids page-cache churn on clean DBs). Idempotent because `redact_for_log`'s `***REDACTED***` sentinel doesn't match any of the 5 redaction patterns. Called in `startup()` BEFORE `_load_jobs_from_db()` so unredacted data never reaches the in-memory `_jobs` dict.
      **Edge cases covered:**
      - Corrupt JSON rows skipped gracefully — one bad row can't kill the whole backfill. `_load_jobs_from_db` already warns about corrupt rows downstream; the backfill defers to that path.
      - Clean rows (already-redacted or never-dirty) are NOT rewritten — keeps the sqlite page cache stable. The explicit `if redacted != orig` check is load-bearing for this property.
      - DB doesn't exist yet (first run) — backfill no-ops via early return on `DB_PATH.exists()`.
      - Idempotent re-run — safe to invoke on every restart (current design choice). Future optimization: add a version table to skip on subsequent restarts, but YAGNI at current scale.
      **Tests (+7) in new `jang-server/tests/test_db_backfill_redaction.py`:**
      - `test_backfill_redacts_hf_token_in_error` — seed row with `hf_*` token in error, verify redaction.
      - `test_backfill_redacts_webhook_secret_in_phase_detail` — seed Slack webhook URL, verify path+query stripped but host kept.
      - `test_backfill_is_idempotent` — run twice, verify 2nd run is a no-op.
      - `test_backfill_leaves_clean_rows_untouched` — verify the "only write changed rows" optimization.
      - `test_backfill_handles_corrupt_rows_gracefully` — corrupt JSON row doesn't kill the sweep for other rows.
      - `test_backfill_noop_when_db_does_not_exist` — first-run safety.
      - `test_backfill_wired_into_startup` — source-inspection pin that `_backfill_redact_persisted_logs()` is called BEFORE `_load_jobs_from_db()` in the startup hook. Without this ordering pin, a future edit could move the backfill after the load, leaving the in-memory `_jobs` dict carrying unredacted strings until the next save.
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:532-591` (backfill function), `:880-888` (startup wiring). 68/68 jang-server tests pass (was 61, +7).
      **Meta-lesson — code-clean ≠ data-clean. Redaction-at-write protects FUTURE rows but not PAST ones.** A naive read of "we redact secrets now" assumes write-time wrapping solves the problem. It doesn't: the existing rows on disk still have the pre-fix data. **Rule: when a security hardening is added to a write path, audit whether any previously-written data has the old vulnerable shape. If so, schedule a backfill to bring data-at-rest up to spec. Code-clean + data-dirty = partial fix + false confidence.** Inverse of iter-117 M182's "repo-wide source sweep" lesson: source can be cleaned in one commit, but data-at-rest has latency.
      **Meta-lesson — startup backfills need ordering pins, not just existence pins.** If the startup hook is `_load_jobs_from_db(); _backfill()`, the backfill still redacts the DB — but the in-memory `_jobs` dict that was already populated has unredacted strings, and it's served to callers via `GET /jobs` until the next write. **Rule: for data-at-rest backfills that feed an in-memory cache, pin the ordering via source-inspection test. The backfill MUST run before the load. Missing this pin lets a future "clean up the startup sequence" refactor silently break the property.** Parallels iter-127 M190's missing-function sanity assert: explicitly pin the relationship the test reader might not think to check.
      **Meta-lesson — idempotency is the unsung backbone of operational safety.** Making `redact_for_log` idempotent in iter-130 (test `test_redact_for_log_idempotent`) paid off here: we can run the backfill on every startup without worrying about double-redacted strings. If the helper weren't idempotent, we'd need a version table + tracking + careful coordination. **Rule: when designing security/data-transform helpers, make idempotency a first-class design requirement, not an afterthought. The cost is usually a well-chosen sentinel pattern; the payoff is flexibility at every later integration point.**
      **Meta-lesson — "only write changed rows" is cheap and important.** A naive backfill would rewrite every row whether or not anything changed, causing unnecessary sqlite page churn on large DBs. The explicit `if redacted != orig` check before `updates.append(...)` keeps clean rows truly untouched. Same 3 lines of code, massive difference at scale. **Rule: when a transform is conditional (some rows change, some don't), let the conditionality propagate to the write path. Conditional writes > unconditional writes, always.**
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M194 (jang-server HTTPException response-body redaction + check_rate_limit `log` shadow cleanup)** — Iter-131 closed the last credential-leak boundary after SOURCE (M182), URL (M192), LOG (M193). HTTPException `detail=` strings are visible to the CALLING CLIENT, not the operator — different attacker access path than logs. M193's `redact_for_log` helper now also applies at response-body formulation sites.
      **Leak vectors identified:**
      - `POST /jobs` (server.py:884): `HTTPException(404, f"Model '{req.model_id}' not found on HuggingFace: {e}")`. `{e}` is an unsanitized HF client exception that sometimes embeds the failing URL with `?use_auth_token=...` query fragments.
      - `POST /estimate` (server.py:1155): `HTTPException(404, f"Model not found: {e}")`. Same leak class, same exception source.
      - `GET /recommend/{model_id:path}` (server.py:1208): same again.
      **Why it matters even though endpoints require auth** (iter-114 M179): an authenticated caller with a LOW-PRIV API key might not deserve to see HF-token fragments from the server's HF_UPLOAD_TOKEN. Response-body redaction is the last line of defense at the trust boundary between server and client.
      **Cleanup scope:** also fixed the iter-130 forecast item — `check_rate_limit` (server.py:371) previously had `log = _rate_limit_log.setdefault(ip, deque())` which shadowed the module-level `log` logger. No current bug (no logger calls inside the function), but any future maintainer adding debug logging would hit `AttributeError: 'collections.deque' object has no attribute 'info'`. Renamed to `ip_log`. All `log.popleft()` / `log[0]` / `log.append(now)` references updated.
      **Tests (+4) in new `jang-server/tests/test_response_body_redaction.py`:**
      - `test_model_not_found_in_jobs_post_is_redacted` — pin redact_for_log around the POST /jobs HF-model-missing 404.
      - `test_model_not_found_in_estimate_is_redacted` — pin all 'Model not found' 404 sites (loops over regex matches for resilience if the phrase appears multiple times).
      - `test_rate_limit_log_renamed_from_log_shadow` — pin the `log = _rate_limit_log...` shadow isn't re-introduced. Uses `re.search(r"\blog\s*=\s*_rate_limit_log")` with word boundary so the replacement `ip_log = _rate_limit_log...` doesn't trivially contain the forbidden substring.
      - `test_redact_for_log_is_used_in_http_exception_bodies` — generic sweep over all HF-client HTTPException raises to catch future new sites that forget the wrapper.
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:371-382` (rename), `jang-server/server.py:889`, `:1161`, `:1215` (redact wraps). 61/61 jang-server tests pass (was 57, +4).
      **Meta-lesson — four-boundary credential hygiene: SOURCE / URL / LOG / RESPONSE.** Each boundary has its own attacker-access profile:
      - SOURCE (M182): repo-read attacker (leaked git clone).
      - URL (M192): URL-intercept attacker (shell history, browser history, proxy log at access time).
      - LOG (M193): log-read attacker (operator with log file access).
      - RESPONSE (M194): API caller (authenticated client or, if auth fails, the 401/403 body).
      Each layer catches a different class of leak + gap. **Rule: for credential hygiene, inventory the FORMATTING boundaries where strings-with-potential-secrets cross a trust line, and apply redaction at each.** Missing any layer creates a silent gap. Parallels iter-114's multi-endpoint auth discovery (auth was on POSTs only; GETs were silently unprotected). Iter-131 closes the 4th corner of the credential-hygiene square.
      **Meta-lesson — substring matching is NOT word matching.** My test `assert "log = _rate_limit_log.setdefault" not in body` failed after the rename because `ip_log = _rate_limit_log.setdefault` CONTAINS `log = _rate_limit_log.setdefault` as a literal substring (the `log` at position 3 of `ip_log` matches the start of the forbidden pattern). Python's `in` operator is substring-match, not word-match. **Rule: when a pattern needs to exclude an old identifier but the replacement shares a suffix/prefix with the old, always use regex with word boundaries (`\b`), not Python's `in`.** Parallels iter-127 M190's structural-slicing lesson: precise anchors > coarse anchors.
      **Meta-lesson — rename cleanups are low-cost, high-value "while you're here" work.** The `log` shadow wasn't a bug today but was a 2-line fix that prevents a hard-to-diagnose AttributeError in some future iter. Cleanups of this shape (pre-buggy naming, shadows, unused imports, dead branches) ride alongside security-scoped iters for ~free and reduce accumulation of pre-bug smells. **Rule: when a nearby observation is ~zero-cost to fix and prevents a future trap, fix it. Don't batch every observation into a cleanup-only iter — that loses the "caught in context" momentum.** Inverse of "don't fix unrelated code during a feature" — that rule is about SCOPE CREEP (big refactors inside feature PRs); this is about OPPORTUNISTIC FIXES (small naming cleanups noticed in passing).
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M193 (jang-server runtime-log secret redaction — covers subprocess stream + webhook URLs + exception traces)** — Iter-130 closed the second-most-common token leak vector after URLs: LOGS. Iter-117 M182 covers SOURCE (hardcoded secrets), iter-129 M192 covers URLs (query-param auth). This iter covers RUNTIME — log lines at format-time, exception storage, and subprocess stdout forwarding.
      **Leak vectors identified:**
      1. `_LogCapture.write()` forwarded subprocess stdout/stderr verbatim via `self.job.log(line)`. convert_model.py is a long pipeline that calls huggingface_hub + transformers + mlx_lm internally — any exception in those layers embeds the failing URL (often with token in query string) in the traceback, and the traceback leaks into both `job.log_lines` (exposed via `GET /jobs/{id}/logs`) AND the module logger (operator access logs).
      2. `"Webhook delivered to {job.webhook_url}"` logged the URL verbatim. Slack/Discord/generic webhook URLs carry the WRITE SECRET in the URL path (e.g., `https://hooks.slack.com/services/T0/B0/XXX` where XXX is the secret). Plain-log = secret leak to anyone with log-read access.
      3. `job.error = f"{e}\n{traceback.format_exc()}"` stored unredacted traceback in the Job model. Exposed via `GET /jobs/{id}`, visible via `GET /jobs/{id}/logs`. HF client raises with the failing URL in exception messages — sometimes with `?use_auth_token=...` query fragments.
      4. 4 `log.warning(...)` calls (restore_jobs row error, estimate HF fetch, recommend HF fetch, architecture check) formatted `{_e}` verbatim. Same failure mode.
      **Fix (iter 130):** added `redact_for_log(s)` helper with 5 layered patterns:
      - `hf_[A-Za-z0-9_-]{20,}` → `hf_***REDACTED***` (matches iter-117 M182's HF shape)
      - `sk-(?:proj-)?[A-Za-z0-9_-]{20,}` → `sk-***REDACTED***` (OpenAI)
      - `Bearer [A-Za-z0-9_\-\.]{16,}` → `Bearer ***REDACTED***` (HTTP auth header in stringified errors)
      - `https://(hooks.slack.com|discord(app)?.com/api/webhooks)[^\s]*` → `https://host/***REDACTED***` (keeps host for diagnostics, nukes secret path)
      - `[?&](api_key|token|access_token|auth)=[^&\s]+` → `key=***REDACTED***` (strips secret-bearing query strings anywhere in any URL)
      **Applied at 7 high-risk sites:** `_LogCapture.write` (wraps every subprocess line), gate-passthrough branch (same stream, secondary handler), webhook delivery success log, webhook delivery failure log, job FAILED log + error storage + phase_detail, 4 `log.warning` sites for HF/restore/architecture failures.
      **Explicitly NOT applied to** low-risk logs (queue start, "Loaded N jobs", static liveness-style messages). Over-redaction hides legit debugging info (model IDs, profile names, file paths). The helper is targeted, not blanket.
      **Tests (+11) in new `jang-server/tests/test_runtime_log_redaction.py`:**
      - 6 unit tests: each redaction pattern (HF, OpenAI, Bearer, Slack, Discord, query-string) + 2 semantics tests (idempotency, clean messages preserved).
      - 3 source-inspection pins: `_LogCapture.write` uses `redact_for_log`, "Webhook delivered to" log wraps with `redact_for_log`, `traceback.format_exc()` storage is wrapped. Each uses `rfind` to anchor on the code site rather than the M193 rationale block at the top of the file.
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:99-152` (helper), plus 7 call-site wrapper edits. 57/57 jang-server tests pass (was 46, +11).
      **Meta-lesson — layered secret-leak defenses: SOURCE → URL → LOG.** Three layers, three sweeps, three invariants:
      - **M182 (iter 117)** — SOURCE sweep: grep the repo for `hf_[A-Za-z0-9_-]{20,}` et al. Catches hardcoded credentials.
      - **M192 (iter 129)** — URL audit: query-param auth restricted to GET, docs hygiene. Catches token-in-URL leak vector.
      - **M193 (iter 130)** — LOG redaction: wrapper at log sites. Catches token-in-log leak vector.
      Each layer catches a different class of bug and a different attacker access path (repo-read, log-read, browser-history-read). **Rule: for credential hygiene, design layered defenses at each format/boundary — source, transit, at-rest storage, logs. A single "don't leak secrets" invariant isn't enough; each layer needs its own pattern and its own test.** Missing any layer creates a gap.
      **Meta-lesson — targeted redaction > blanket redaction.** A naive implementation might apply `redact_for_log` to every `log.info/warning` call or wrap the logger at the basicConfig level. That hides legit debugging info AND makes the security property invisible (reviewer can't tell which logs are sensitive without reading the wrapper). **Rule: apply redaction at the specific sites that touch untrusted strings (subprocess output, user-supplied URLs, exception messages). Keep clean-origin logs (static strings, counters, known-safe identifiers) unwrapped. The test suite then pins the sites that MUST have the wrapper, keeping the security property visible and auditable.** Same philosophy as iter-114's "auth on sensitive endpoints, not all" — precision over blanket.
      **Meta-lesson — source-inspection tests must anchor past their own rationale comments.** My first-pass `test_webhook_delivery_log_uses_redact_for_log` used `content.find("Webhook delivered to")` and found the phrase in MY OWN rationale comment at the top of the file — 5 screens above the actual code site. Fix: `content.rfind(...)` to anchor on the LAST occurrence (the code site, since comments come first). **Rule: source-inspection tests that pin a specific call site must be resilient to future comments that quote the code. Use `rfind` when the rationale lives above the code, or switch to a more precise anchor like "`job.log(f\"Webhook delivered`" (includes the log call prefix).** The latter is more robust; the former is simpler.
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M192 (jang-server query-param auth restricted to GET + docs sweep)** — Iter-129 follow-on to M191's CORS thinking. Pre-M192 `check_auth` accepted `?api_key=<token>` on ANY HTTP method. Tokens in non-GET URLs leak into: (1) server access logs (uvicorn + nginx log the full URL at INFO by default), (2) browser history (curl-from-shared-workstation risk), (3) proxy/CDN logs (Cloudflare, nginx — log query strings by default), (4) terminal history (`curl` saved to `.bash_history`). Non-GET auth-in-URL is the most common "legit accidental token leak" source in Python web apps.
      **Fix (iter 129):**
      - `check_auth` now reads `?api_key=` **only on GET requests** (SSE/EventSource compatibility — browsers' EventSource API can't set custom headers).
      - POST / DELETE / PATCH requests without a Bearer header → fail-closed to empty token → 401 with a message naming `Authorization: Bearer <key>` as the preferred scheme.
      - Docstring explains the leak vectors + the EventSource carve-out so a future maintainer doesn't "clean up" the branch and break browser SSE clients.
      - API.md (iter-128-adjacent docs hygiene): fixed stale post-M179 protected/unprotected endpoint list (every GET is now protected — was listed as "Unprotected" in docs), replaced `hf_MGS...` partial-token fragment in the HF_UPLOAD_TOKEN default column with `(empty)`, added explicit "GET requests only" annotation on the query-param auth method.
      **Tests (+6) in new `jang-server/tests/test_query_param_auth_restricted.py`:**
      - `test_check_auth_reads_query_param_only_on_get` — pin the `method == "GET"` gate on the query_params branch.
      - `test_check_auth_non_get_rejects_missing_header` — pin fail-closed empty-string assignment so non-GET non-Bearer doesn't silently read query_params.
      - `test_check_auth_error_message_points_at_the_right_scheme` — 401 message names `Authorization: Bearer`.
      - `test_check_auth_docstring_explains_risk` — docstring mentions log-leak + EventSource carve-out (prevents well-meaning future cleanup that removes the GET branch).
      - `test_docs_api_md_documents_get_only_restriction` — API.md documents the restriction; without it, client authors won't know and will POST tokens in URLs.
      - `test_docs_api_md_has_no_token_fragment` — no `hf_<≥2 chars>` literals in API.md (even 3-char prefixes confirm the token format and narrow an attacker's search).
      **Why the `hf_MGS...` fragment mattered:** it was the literal prefix of the iter-116-leaked HF_UPLOAD_TOKEN (`hf_MGSmwyHPzKFd...`). Repo-wide M182 sweep requires `{20,}` length so 3-char fragments slipped through. A scanner correlating the fragment against a leak database could pinpoint the same token. Cheapest fix: remove the fragment from docs + add the 2-char-minimum docs test. The broader M182 test stays at 20+ chars for code (avoids false positives on `hf_abc` style illustrations); the docs test is tighter because docs are for humans and don't need illustrative shapes.
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:642-672`, `jang-server/docs/API.md:72-95`. 46/46 jang-server tests pass (was 40, +6).
      **Meta-lesson — auth-in-URL is the most common "legit accidental token leak" vector.** Every operator who uses `curl http://api/endpoint?api_key=ABC` has now committed their key to shell history. Every browser nav to `GET /stream?api_key=ABC` has now recorded the key in the browser's URL bar + history store. Every nginx/Cloudflare in front of the API has logged it at access-log level. **Rule: for web APIs, prefer Authorization header exclusively. Query-param auth is a narrow compat concession for specific browser APIs (EventSource) — restrict it by HTTP method or endpoint path, and never allow it on write methods (POST/PUT/DELETE/PATCH).** The non-obvious corollary: even with TLS encrypting the URL in transit, the server and all intermediate proxies still log the URL in plaintext at rest.
      **Meta-lesson — docs hygiene is part of the security audit surface.** Pre-M192 API.md had three independently-bad-but-cheap-to-fix issues: (a) stale post-M179 endpoint list, (b) partial token fragment, (c) query-param auth listed as equal-status with Bearer. Each would have been easy to miss in a code-only audit. **Rule: when auditing security posture, include the docs in the diff. Stale docs mislead client authors into insecure patterns; fragments narrow attacker searches; feature-equality listing encourages the less-safe path.** Doc-scope tests (like `test_docs_api_md_has_no_token_fragment`) are a cheap, high-value layer.
      **Meta-lesson — preserve the narrow legit use case explicitly in the docstring.** Without the "EventSource/SSE compatibility" carve-out in the check_auth docstring, a future iter-140 audit could see "query-param auth allowed on GET — why?" and remove it as dead-weight, silently breaking browser SSE clients. The docstring + the `test_check_auth_docstring_explains_risk` pin together ensure the carve-out's rationale survives future refactors. **Rule: when a security restriction has a narrow legit exception, DOCUMENT the exception's rationale in the code where it's applied — and add a test that pins the documentation.** The test turns the rationale into a hard requirement for any future edit.
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M191 (jang-server CORS posture — tightened from wildcard to env-driven allowlist)** — Iter-128 closed the CORS finding first noticed during iter-126's M189 work. Pre-M191 the server shipped with `allow_origins=["*"]`, `allow_methods=["*"]`, and `allow_headers=["*"]` — a permissive-by-default stance that let any origin send any method with any header and (pre-M114) read responses. After iter-114 M179 moved auth onto every endpoint, credential-based CSRF is partially dampened by CORSMiddleware's default `allow_credentials=False` (browsers strip cookies/auth headers on wildcard origins), BUT:
      1. **Response reads still leak** to any origin via endpoints that leak data via error messages (a crafted malformed body can trigger a Pydantic error message containing path fragments or environment hints).
      2. **Query-param auth (`?api_key=<token>`) is outside the cookie strip** — a malicious page in the victim's browser can't force the request (browser won't send credentials with wildcard origin on credentialed calls), but CAN issue simple requests with query-param auth if the attacker has the API key through any other leak path.
      3. **Principle of least privilege** — restrict by default, require operators to opt into broader access.
      **Fix (iter 128):**
      - Added `JANG_CORS_ORIGINS` env var (default `"http://localhost,http://127.0.0.1"`). Parsed into `CORS_ORIGINS` list by comma-split + strip. Explicit `"*"` still supported as an opt-in for public APIs.
      - Added `CORS_METHODS = ["GET", "POST", "DELETE", "OPTIONS"]` — exactly the methods the server actually uses + OPTIONS for preflight. Rejects PATCH/PUT/TRACE/CONNECT which no route handles.
      - Added `CORS_HEADERS = ["Content-Type", "Authorization"]` — only what the `check_auth` dependency reads (`Authorization: Bearer …`) and what clients use to send JSON (`Content-Type`). No `X-Foo` custom headers needed by any route.
      - Middleware registration now references these constants instead of inline `["*"]` literals.
      **Tests (+6) in new `jang-server/tests/test_cors_posture.py`:**
      - `test_cors_origins_env_var_defined` — env var name exists in source.
      - `test_cors_origins_default_is_not_wildcard` — default ≠ `"*"` + includes localhost.
      - `test_cors_origins_list_is_parsed_from_env` — comma-split actually performed; `CORS_ORIGINS` constant exists.
      - `test_cors_methods_is_not_wildcard` — `CORS_METHODS` ≠ wildcard + includes GET/POST minimum.
      - `test_cors_headers_is_not_wildcard` — `CORS_HEADERS` ≠ wildcard + includes Authorization minimum.
      - `test_cors_middleware_uses_the_restricted_constants` — middleware registration references `CORS_ORIGINS`/`CORS_METHODS`/`CORS_HEADERS`, not inline literals (prevents a future edit from re-introducing wildcards while leaving the constants-only tests green).
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:668-695`. 40/40 jang-server tests pass (was 34, +6).
      **Meta-lesson — permissive defaults shift security burden onto every operator, forever.** The original `allow_origins=["*"]` was probably written to "just work" during development. The cost: every production deployment inherits an attack surface until the operator remembers to tighten it — and most won't. **Rule: when picking defaults for security-relevant config, pick the RESTRICTIVE choice and require operators to opt in to broader access. Inverse of the "reasonable default" rule from M189 — for security, "reasonable" means "safe", not "permissive".** Industry convention: `nginx default_server` is restrictive; `ssh PermitRootLogin` defaults to `no`; modern web frameworks increasingly ship CSP/CSRF headers on by default. Follow the pattern.
      **Meta-lesson — constants named at module scope make middleware auditable.** Before M191: `allow_origins=["*"]` was an inline literal — a grep for "cors_origins" found nothing because the source had `allow_origins` not `CORS_ORIGINS`, and the literal value was unnamed. After M191: `CORS_ORIGINS = [...]` is a stable, greppable, testable name. The middleware is also more auditable because `app.add_middleware(... allow_origins=CORS_ORIGINS ...)` makes it obvious which CONFIGURED value controls access. **Rule: when a middleware takes configuration values, pull them into named module-level constants. Future auditors (human or automated) can then find, pin, and test them without parsing literal expressions inside function-call sites.**
      **Meta-lesson — tighten methods and headers alongside origins.** I initially thought "fix origins" was the whole fix. Then realized `allow_methods=["*"]` was separately permissive (a malicious origin could still issue PATCH/PUT even with origins tightened, if those methods were ever added to a route without CORS review). Security sweeps on CORS should tighten the full triad — origins, methods, headers — not just one. Each one independently expands or tightens the attack surface. **Rule: when auditing CORS, treat `allow_origins` + `allow_methods` + `allow_headers` as a single configuration unit. Tighten all three in one change.**
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M190 (jang-server M177 invariant allowlist — refactor from line-number range to function-body context)** — Iter-127 closed out the iter-125 "4-bump rule" that fired at iter-126. Background: iter-111's M177 invariant (`test_no_bare_except_exception_pass_in_server`) carries a 1-site allowlist for a legit `except Exception: pass` inside the download-progress tick loop (`bytes_total=0` would raise `ZeroDivisionError` — micro-defense acceptable, noisy logging every tick is not). The original allowlist was a line-number range (`allowed_lines = set(range(1115, 1500))`), which required manual bumps every time the body-above-the-site grew. It bumped 4 times: 1121 (iter-111) → 1150 (iter-124 M187) → 1207 (iter-125 M188) → 1300 (iter-126 M189). Iter-125's meta-lesson had codified the rule: "if a moving-line allowlist bumps 4 times in 4 iters, refactor to structural slicing."
      **Fix (iter 127):** replaced the range with a function-name → rationale dict (`allowed_function_bodies = {"_phase_download": "tick-loop progress-pct guard — bytes_total=0 ZeroDivisionError must not spam logs every tick"}`) and a small parser that scans `server.py` for `def NAME(` / `async def NAME(` lines, computes `(start_line, end_line)` for each allowed function name, and unions those ranges into `allowed_lines`. The parser treats the NEXT top-level `def` or EOF as the end-of-function sentinel, which is fine for non-nested functions (the pass site is inside `_phase_download`'s for-loop but the for-loop body is still inside the def, so the range still matches).
      **Sanity assert:** added a "missing function" check — `missing = set(allowed_function_bodies) - set(func_ranges)` — that fails loudly if `_phase_download` is renamed/removed. Without this, a rename would silently drop the allowlist and fail the invariant for a legitimate site; WORSE, a future `except Exception: pass` could be added to a totally different function and the allowlist wouldn't catch it because the test would no longer report which function-body contained the offender. The assert also points the maintainer at the right action (update the key if renamed, drop + audit if removed).
      **What the 4-bump rule buys us:** immunity to line-number shifts as long as the bare-pass stays inside `_phase_download`. Any future iter adding 50 lines above the site (like M189 did) is now a no-op on this test. If the pass is ever MOVED to a different function, the test rightly fails — flagging the maintainer to audit whether the new site deserves the allowlist.
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/tests/test_exception_handling_invariant.py:42-112`. 34/34 jang-server tests pass.
      **Meta-lesson — structural slicing > line-number ranges for long-lived allowlists.** Line numbers are the most brittle identifier a test can anchor to; they shift with every edit above the target site. Function names are stable across all reasonable refactors (a rename is intentional and should invalidate the allowlist entry). Anchoring to structural names (function, class, module) produces an invariant that survives churn without maintenance. **Rule: any allowlist that needs to target a specific code site — always anchor by function/class/module name, never by line number. Line numbers are only acceptable for ONE-SHOT test fixtures that regenerate; they're lethal for invariants that live for months.**
      **Meta-lesson — the 4-bump rule is the right "refactor now" threshold.** Under 4 bumps, the allowlist has paid its cost but each bump is cheap (edit two numbers, re-run 1 test). At 4 bumps, you've paid the cost 4× AND proven the line-drift is inevitable. Refactoring costs ~30 lines of parser + 1 sanity assert. Amortized over the lifetime of the test (years for a long-lived server), the refactor pays for itself within ~2 more bumps. Under 4, the refactor is premature; over 4, it's overdue. **Rule: 4-bump threshold for moving-line → structural allowlist refactors. Codify in iter-125-style meta-lessons when a test's allowlist requires its 4th manual update; refactor on the 5th bump or preempt at the 4th.**
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M189 (jang-server max-request-body-size middleware — closes memory-bomb DoS)** — Iter-126 closed the last unbounded-input vector in jang-server. Pre-M189 the server had NO upper bound on POST body size. An attacker could send a 10 GB JSON body, exhausting RAM before Pydantic validation rejects the wrong shape. JobRequest / EstimateRequest payloads are at most a few KB in practice (model_id + profile + small metadata), so capping at 1 MB by default leaves 1000× headroom and stops memory-bomb requests cold.
      **Fix (iter 126):** added `MAX_BODY_BYTES` constant (env-tunable via `JANG_MAX_BODY_BYTES`, default 1 MB) + `@app.middleware("http")` `limit_body_size` middleware that:
      1. Reads `Content-Length` header from request.
      2. If `> MAX_BODY_BYTES`, returns 413 Payload Too Large (per RFC 9110) with explanation + env-var name.
      3. If header is malformed/missing, falls through to normal handling (don't second-guess weird headers; downstream Pydantic catches malformed bodies).
      **Coverage:** the header-based check covers the typical attacker who declares the size. A sneaky attacker could use chunked transfer encoding (no Content-Length header) to bypass — full coverage would require streaming-byte-counter middleware, more complex. Acceptable trade-off given (a) most HTTP clients send Content-Length for static-size bodies, (b) chunked-bypass requires the attacker to actually send the bytes, which still costs them bandwidth, (c) reverse proxies (nginx default `client_max_body_size 1m`) can backstop in production.
      **Tests (+5) in new `jang-server/tests/test_max_body_size.py`:**
      - `test_max_body_bytes_env_var_defined`
      - `test_limit_body_size_middleware_registered` — pin the `@app.middleware("http")` decorator.
      - `test_middleware_rejects_with_413` — HTTP status compliance per RFC 9110.
      - `test_middleware_inspects_content_length_header` — pin the header-name lowercase match.
      - `test_default_cap_is_reasonable_for_jang_payloads` — guards against accidental defaults outside [64KB, 100MB].
      Also bumped iter-111 M177 invariant allowlist range AGAIN (line shifted from ~1207 to ~1300 with the body-size middleware's ~50 lines added above). 4th bump now — meta-lesson queued: refactor to function-body slicing per iter-125.
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:668-712`. 34 jang-server tests pass (was 29, +5).
      **Meta-lesson — declared-size body bombs are the cheap-fix half; chunked bypasses are the hard half.** Header-based checks catch 95% of attackers (most HTTP clients always send Content-Length). The remaining 5% (chunked-encoding without declaring size) are advanced and usually need network-layer mitigation (nginx, WAF). For an in-app middleware, header check is the right level of defense — defending against the declared-size case is cheap and high-value; defending against chunked bypass is expensive and usually duplicate of network-layer protections. **Rule: when adding security middleware to a Python web app, check the header path FIRST; backstop chunked bypasses with reverse-proxy config.** Document the layer responsibility so the operator knows what to configure.
      **Meta-lesson — sensible defaults bound the input space.** A 1 MB default cap was chosen because (a) JANG payloads are KB-scale (1000× headroom), (b) operators rarely need to send larger payloads to a quantization API, (c) 1 MB is the de facto default in nginx + IIS + most reverse proxies — operators are used to it. Picking a default that matches industry conventions reduces "why does my legit upload fail?" surprises. **Rule: when adding a configurable cap, anchor the default to industry conventions; document the conventions in the comment.**
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M188 (jang-server SSE concurrent-connection cap — complements M187)** — Iter-125 closes a DoS gap iter-124 M187's rate-limiter doesn't cover. M187 caps the OPEN-call rate (e.g., 30 streams/minute per IP). But SSE streams are LONG-LIVED — a client can open at the rate limit and accumulate thousands of open streams over time. Each consumes a file descriptor + asyncio task + _sse_subscribers entry. Process FD limits (typically 1024-4096 default on macOS/Linux) become the real cap; hitting them bricks the whole server.
      **Fix (iter 125):** added per-IP + global concurrent-stream caps. Two env vars: `JANG_SSE_MAX_PER_IP` (default 10) + `JANG_SSE_MAX_GLOBAL` (default 200). Stream open path:
      1. Acquire `_sse_count_lock`.
      2. Check global open count (sum of all IP counts) against `SSE_MAX_GLOBAL` — reject 429 if exceeded.
      3. Check per-IP count against `SSE_MAX_PER_IP` — reject 429 if exceeded.
      4. Increment IP counter.
      5. Release lock + proceed to setup queue + return StreamingResponse.
      **Pair with decrement** in event_generator's `finally` block. Drops the dict key when count hits 0 (matches iter-115 M180 ghost-key pattern). Without the matching decrement, a client who opens N streams + closes them ALL would still show N in the per-IP count, locking them out of new streams forever — silent monotonic accumulation bug.
      **429 messages** explain WHICH cap was hit (per-IP vs global) so the client can react appropriately. Per-IP says "close existing streams or wait"; global says "try again in a minute" (other clients holding streams might close).
      **Tests (+4) in new `jang-server/tests/test_sse_connection_limit.py`:**
      - `test_sse_connection_caps_defined` — env vars + rationale defined.
      - `test_stream_job_checks_ip_count_before_accept` — preamble checks both caps.
      - `test_event_generator_decrements_counter_in_finally` — pin the decrement + dict-cleanup so the M180 ghost-key class can't reappear here.
      - `test_sse_caps_documented_in_source` — comments mention "file descriptor" so future tuning sees the WHY.
      **Test gotcha caught + fixed:** initial test substring-search window was 3000 chars from the function start; the decrement lives past that. Bumped to 5000 with a comment explaining the function's grown body (M188 preamble + M180 cleanup + M188 decrement). Iter-104 M108 / iter-115's "moving line numbers" lesson applied to substring-window sizes too.
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:262-280, 901-940, 974-980`. 29 jang-server tests pass (was 25, +4).
      **Meta-lesson — open-rate limit ≠ concurrent-count limit.** Two distinct DoS vectors with two distinct mitigations:
       - **Open-rate** (iter-124 M187): how fast can a client establish new connections? → token bucket / sliding window.
       - **Concurrent-count** (iter-125 M188): how many open connections can a client hold simultaneously? → counter + cap.
      Long-lived connections (SSE, WebSocket, gRPC streams) need BOTH. Short-lived requests (typical REST POST/GET) only need open-rate. **Rule for any new endpoint: classify connection lifetime — long-lived needs both limits, short-lived needs only the rate limit.**
      **Meta-lesson — paired increment/decrement state needs explicit pin tests.** The bug "monotonic counter never decrements" is exactly the kind of thing that PASSES every functional test until your client hits SSE_MAX_PER_IP after a few hours of normal usage. The pin test asserts `_sse_open_counts[ip]` AND `- 1` appear in the function body — catches a future refactor that drops the decrement. Same M180 class as the ghost-key cleanup.
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M187 (jang-server rate-limiting on POST /estimate + POST /jobs)** — Iter-124 added a per-IP sliding-window rate limiter to jang-server's high-cost POST endpoints. Pre-M187 a single client could flood:
      - **POST /estimate** — each call hits the HF API (`HfApi.model_info`). An auth'd attacker could exhaust the server's HF rate-limit budget, breaking `/estimate` for every other user.
      - **POST /jobs** — creates DB rows + runs validation (HFRepoValidator, duplicate detection, per-user limit check). MAX_JOBS_PER_USER bounds CONCURRENT jobs but not creation RATE — flooding rejected submissions still consumes CPU.
      MAX_CONCURRENT bounds total compute work, but neither endpoint had per-source rate limiting.
      **Fix (iter 124):** added `check_rate_limit(request: Request) -> None` FastAPI dependency. Sliding-window per-IP, configurable via `JANG_RATE_LIMIT_WINDOW_S` (default 60s) + `JANG_RATE_LIMIT_MAX_REQUESTS` (default 30 = avg 1 req every 2s). Implementation: `dict[ip, deque[timestamps]]` with `popleft` for window expiry — sliding-window not fixed-counter (fixed counter would let clients burst at minute boundaries).
      Rate-check fires BEFORE auth check (via `dependencies=[Depends(check_rate_limit), Depends(check_auth)]`) so failed-auth attempts also count against the limit — defends against auth-brute-force flooding too.
      **429 response** carries `Retry-After` header with exact remaining seconds — matches RFC 6585.
      **Public endpoints** (`/health`, `/profiles`) explicitly NOT rate-limited (test pins this) — keeps liveness probes cheap. Hostile flooding of `/health` is a network-layer concern (nginx/WAF).
      **Tests (+5) in new `jang-server/tests/test_rate_limit.py`:**
      - `test_check_rate_limit_function_exists`
      - `test_check_rate_limit_uses_sliding_window` — pins `popleft` + `_rate_limit_log` literals so fixed-counter regressions fail.
      - `test_rate_limit_applied_to_high_cost_endpoints` — parser asserts /estimate + /jobs include `check_rate_limit` Depends.
      - `test_public_endpoints_skip_rate_limit` — guards over-correction.
      - `test_rate_limit_env_vars_documented` — env-var names appear in source for operator discoverability.
      Also widened iter-111 M177 allowlist range (line numbers shifted again from ~1150 to ~1207 due to rate-limit helper's ~50 lines added above).
      **Evidence:** `jang-server/server.py:262-318, 633, 928`. 25 jang-server tests pass (was 20, +5).
      **Meta-lesson — rate limits should fire BEFORE auth checks.** Putting `Depends(check_rate_limit)` before `Depends(check_auth)` means failed-auth attempts count against the limit too. This defends against auth-brute-force flooding (each guess costs the attacker rate-budget). Reverse order would let an attacker spray invalid keys at infinite rate. **Rule: in any FastAPI app, rate-limit deps must come BEFORE auth deps.**
      **Meta-lesson — sliding window > fixed counter.** A fixed-counter ("at most N per minute, resets at minute :00") lets a client send N at :59 and another N at :00:01 = 2N in 2 seconds. Sliding window ("at most N in any 60s span") prevents the burst. Trivial impl difference (`deque + popleft` vs `int + time.minute`) but big behavioral difference. **Rule: when implementing rate limits, use sliding-window from the start.**
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M186 (JANGQuantizer.swiftpm QueueView — Cancel/Retry buttons silently swallowed errors)** — Iter-121 continued the JANGQuantizer.swiftpm sweep started in iter-120 M185. Found 2 more iter-35 M107-class silent-swallows in QueueView.swift's job-card action buttons:
      ```swift
      Button("Cancel") { Task { try? await api.cancelJob(job.jobId) } }
      Button("Retry") { Task { try? await api.retryJob(job.jobId) } }
      ```
      The `try?` consumes any failure (server unreachable, auth expired, network blip, the targeted job no longer in a cancellable state). User clicks Cancel → fails → button looks like nothing happened → user clicks again, gets the same nothing. iter-35 M107 / iter-90 M167 / iter-120 M185 pattern in yet another fresh file (third instance of the same pattern across two apps + two views).
      **Fix (iter 121):** swapped `try?` → `do/catch`, added `@State actionError: String?` to JobCard. Catch sets `actionError = "Cancel failed: \(error.localizedDescription)"` (uses iter-91 M168's actionable-description chain — APIError.errorDescription already follows that pattern from M185). Renders inline below the action buttons in red so the user sees the diagnostic on the same card without leaving the queue list. Success path nulls actionError so the error message clears on a retry.
      **Tests:** SwiftPM has no XCTest harness; visual review only. M182 secrets sweep still clean (78 ralph_runner tests pass).
      **Other audits in this iter (no bugs found):**
      - QueueView refresh timer: properly invalidated on .onDisappear ✓ (iter-94 M171 pattern correctly applied here).
      - QueueView.refresh() ad-hoc Task: comment says "Keep existing data on refresh failure" — intentional silent-fall-through, acceptable per iter-104 M108 taxonomy bucket. Mostly benign for a 3-second poll cycle.
      - SubmitView.submit Task: ad-hoc no handle, but submission is a brief HTTP call (<1s typical). Polish item, not a bug.
      **Meta-lesson — third instance of a pattern means it needs a feedback memory.** iter-35 M107 (JANGStudio Settings) → iter-120 M185 (JANGQuantizer Settings) → iter-121 M186 (JANGQuantizer Queue). Three distinct files in two apps shipped with `} catch { swallow }` for user-action buttons. **Rule for the team: any user-action button calling an async API must use `do/catch` + visible error surface. NEVER `try?` in a Button handler unless the operation is truly idempotent + best-effort (e.g., dismissing a notification).** Should codify this as a feedback memory note alongside the iter-83 pipe-drain + iter-92 remediation memos. Recurrence pattern: same dev, same blind spot, every fresh button.
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M185 (JANGQuantizer.swiftpm — URL query injection in listJobs + silent health-check error in SettingsView)** — Iter-120 audited the previously-unaudited `jang-server/frontend/JANGQuantizer.swiftpm/` Swift Package (jang-server's standalone client UI). Found two bugs in the first pass — different classes, both real, both standard fixes from the iter-83/92/94/101 patterns I've been refining.
      **Bug A — URL query injection in `APIClient.listJobs` (APIClient.swift:49):**
      ```swift
      var path = "/jobs?"
      if let u = user, !u.isEmpty { path += "user=\(u)&" }
      if let p = phase, !p.isEmpty { path += "phase=\(p)&" }
      ```
      String concatenation without URL encoding. A username containing `&`, `=`, `?`, `#`, `+`, space, or `%` breaks the URL OR injects parameters. Example: user named `alice&phase=COMPLETED` → server receives `user=alice` AND `phase=COMPLETED` (overriding any phase the caller intended). Not adversarial in the usual SSRF sense (user names are typically benign) but a real data-correctness bug.
      **Fix:** rebuilt with `URLComponents` + `URLQueryItem`. URLSession encodes correctly. Defensive `guard let pathPlusQuery = components.string` surfaces composition failures clearly.
      **Bug B — silent health-check error in `SettingsView.Check Connection` button:**
      ```swift
      Button("Check Connection") { Task { do { health = try await api.getHealth() } catch { health = nil } } }
      ```
      Classic iter-35 M107 / iter-90 M167 silent-swallow pattern in a fresh file. User clicks "Check Connection" → server unreachable / wrong URL / bad token → `health = nil` → status indicator simply disappears with NO error message. User has zero diagnostic for what failed.
      **Fix:** added `@State private var lastError: String?`. Catch branch sets `lastError = "Connection failed: \(error.localizedDescription)"` (uses iter-91 M168 / iter-92 M169 actionable-description pattern from `APIError.errorDescription`). Renders in red below the button when non-nil. Initial-load `.task` keeps silent-fail behavior (server-not-yet-started shouldn't show a banner on first app open) — only the user-clicked button surfaces the error.
      **Tests:** the swiftpm has no XCTest harness; visual code review + re-running M182 sweep (78 ralph_runner tests pass; the new edits are clean per the secrets sweep).
      **Meta-lesson — patterns identified in one app surface in fresh apps the same way.** iter-35 M107 (Settings silent-swallow) was fixed in JANGStudio months ago. JANGQuantizer.swiftpm is a younger, smaller app written by the same team — and shipped with the same anti-pattern. **Rule: when a meta-pattern is established for one app, sweep ALL apps in the monorepo for the same pattern.** Iter-117 M182's repo-wide approach for secrets is the same idea applied at the test level; iter-120 M185 applies it as a code-pattern review.
      **Meta-lesson — string concatenation for URL query strings is an evergreen bug.** Even in 2025 Swift code with the `URLComponents` API readily available, devs reach for the simpler `+= "param=\(value)&"` pattern. **Rule: any URL query construction in any HTTP client must use `URLComponents` + `URLQueryItem`. Banner this in the JANGQuantizer code review checklist (and in feedback memory if a session-spanning rule is warranted).**
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M184 (M182 sweep was scanning 569 generated files in `.build/` dirs — SKIP_DIR_NAMES gap)** — Iter-119 was originally scoped as "extend coverage to HTML/JS for jang-server frontend." Found that the frontend is a Swift Package (`.swiftpm`) — Swift files already covered by M182. While inspecting coverage, ran a diagnostic that revealed M182's `_iter_source_files` was scanning **569 files inside `JANGQuantizer.swiftpm/.build/` build outputs every test run** — generated code that has no business being audited. Slowed the test ~5× AND risked false positives from compiler-generated identifiers that happen to shape-match secret regexes.
      **Root cause:** `SKIP_DIR_NAMES` had `"build"` (lowercase, no dot) but the SwiftPM output dir is named `.build` with a leading dot. `Path.parts` matches whole components — `.build` doesn't match `build` even with substring logic.
      **Fix (iter 119):** added `.build` to SKIP_DIR_NAMES with an explanatory comment. Pre-emptively also added `.pytest_cache`, `.mypy_cache`, `.ruff_cache`, `.tox` (other common dotted build/cache dirs that could appear in future).
      **Trap I almost set:** initially also added `.swiftpm` to the skip set — would have skipped the legit JANGQuantizer Sources because they live INSIDE `JANGQuantizer.swiftpm/Sources/`. The `.swiftpm` is a CONTAINER directory (like `.app`), not a build output. Backed out + added a NOTE comment so the next maintainer doesn't make the same mistake.
      **Verified:** 7 SwiftPM Sources still covered; 0 files in `.build/` dirs scanned post-fix.
      **Tests:** 78 ralph_runner tests pass (count unchanged — fix is in skip-set, not new test assertions).
      **Meta-lesson — diagnostic checks reveal infrastructure bugs that the test logic itself can't catch.** M182's `test_no_hardcoded_secrets_repo_wide` PASSED before iter-119 — there were no secrets in the .build/ files (it's compiled output, no string literals in human-readable form). The bug was performance + risk-of-future-FP, not correctness. **Rule: when designing an exclusion-based test (skip these dirs / extensions / patterns), periodically print what the test IS scanning to confirm the exclusion logic matches intent. Cheap diagnostic; catches the "wrong dir name" / "case mismatch" / "missing entry" class of skip-set bug.**
      **Meta-lesson — dotted build dirs need explicit skip entries.** Path-component matching doesn't see `.build` as containing `build`. Same trap will hit `.gradle`, `.cargo`, `.terraform`, etc. **Rule: when adding skip entries, list BOTH dotted and undotted variants for any dir that could appear with either prefix.**
      **Meta-lesson — never assume container-dir extensions are build outputs.** `.swiftpm`, `.app`, `.framework`, `.bundle` are MAC OS BUNDLE conventions — they look like extensions but contain real source. Distinguish "package container" from "build output" before adding to skip list. The note-comment in code prevents a future iter from re-introducing the trap.
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M183 (Extend M182 secrets sweep to non-source files: JSON / YAML / shell / docs)** — Iter-118 extends iter-117 M182 to other file types where hardcoded secrets commonly leak: JSON / YAML configs, shell scripts (deploy + CI), `.env` files, `.toml` configs, `.cfg` files, and Markdown docs (CI logs / examples sometimes embed live tokens).
      **Approach:** new `test_no_hardcoded_secrets_in_nonsource_files` companion test in the same file. Reuses M182's `SECRET_PATTERNS` regex set + `ALLOWED_FIXTURES` allowlist mechanism (deduplicated by adding NONSOURCE_EXTENSIONS + a `_iter_nonsource_files` walker). Skips `pyproject.toml` (package specs may shape-match) and `.env.example` (placeholder values are the file's purpose).
      **First run found 6 hits** — all in our own audit docs (`AUDIT_CHECKLIST.md` + `INVESTIGATION_LOG.md`) where the M181/M182 entries reference fixture token names by literal value. These are audit documentation, not actual leaks. Allowlisted both doc files for both HF regex flavors with rationale.
      **Result:** repo-wide secrets coverage now spans `.py + .swift` (M182) AND `.json + .yaml + .yml + .sh + .env + .md + .toml + .cfg` (M183). 4 file types where the M181 class of bug commonly manifests now have automated coverage.
      **Tests:** 78 ralph_runner tests pass (was 77, +1).
      **Meta-lesson — extension iters are cheap when the test infrastructure is reusable.** M182 built the regex set + allowlist mechanism; M183 reuses both, just adds a different file walker. ~10 min of work; same compound-interest pattern as iter-99→iter-100 (PreflightRunner sourceBytesPerWeight reused in PostConvertVerifier) and iter-105→iter-106 (jang-tools dual-invariant template reused in ralph_runner). **Rule: when an audit invariant is being designed, structure it for reuse FROM THE START — keep the regex/threshold/allowlist mechanisms in one place + invoke them from multiple test functions for different scopes (per-file, per-module, repo-wide-source, repo-wide-config). Saves iters for follow-up extensions.**
      **Meta-lesson — audit documentation is itself a leak surface.** My own AUDIT_CHECKLIST.md / INVESTIGATION_LOG.md entries quoting fixture tokens by literal value showed up as "offenders" in the M183 sweep. Files that are part of a security audit can themselves contain pattern-matching strings. **Rule: when documenting fixture data in markdown, prefer redacted forms (`hf_<lit>...<yz>`) over full literals — keeps the docs portable + sweep-clean. Or, allowlist the docs explicitly with a rationale comment (the path I took here for backwards compat with existing entries).**
      **Commit:** (this iteration)
- [x] **M182 (Repo-wide secrets-sweep invariant — extends M181's per-file check to all .py + .swift)** — Iter-117 extends iter-116 M181's jang-server-scoped secrets check to a cross-cutting invariant covering every `.py` and `.swift` file across the repo. Without repo-wide coverage, the same M181-class bug could reappear in jang-tools, JANGStudio Swift, ralph_runner, jang-runtime, or any future module without anyone noticing until the next manual audit.
      **Patterns covered:** HF (`hf_*`, `huggingface_*`), OpenAI (`sk-*`, `sk-proj-*`), AWS (`AKIA*`, `ASIA*`), GitHub (`gh[pousr]_*`). Standard secret shapes that have file:line-grep-able signatures.
      **Allowlist mechanism:** per-(file, pattern-name) tuples for clearly-fake test fixtures. First run found 3 fixture-shape literals: `hf_literal_looking_token_abc123xyz` + `hf_dummy_token_for_test` in jang-tools/tests/test_publish.py + `huggingface_abcdef_ghij-...` in DiagnosticsBundleTests.swift. All three are existing test data verifying scrub-sensitive / token-disambiguation behavior. Allowlisted with rationale.
      **Test masks the matched substring** in failure output so the assertion error itself doesn't re-leak the hit value (only `[hf_lit<...>yz]`-style preview shown).
      **New file:** `ralph_runner/tests/test_no_hardcoded_secrets_repo_wide.py`. Skips vendored third-party code (build/, .venv/, node_modules/, site-packages/) which has its own test fixtures + public test tokens.
      **Tests pass:** 77 ralph_runner tests pass (was 76, +1).
      **Meta-lesson — per-module invariants don't catch cross-module regressions; build a cross-cutting one.** iter-116 M181 added the secrets check ONLY to jang-server. Without iter-117's repo-wide variant, a future hardcode in jang-tools or JANGStudio would slip through. **Rule: when an invariant catches a bug class that could occur in any source file, scope it repo-wide. Per-module invariants are useful for module-specific patterns (e.g., the iter-104/105/106 dual-invariant for `try?` / `except Exception` taxonomy varies by language); cross-cutting bugs need cross-cutting tests.**
      **Meta-lesson — mask matched secret substrings in failure output.** A test that prints the secret it found in its assertion message effectively re-leaks the secret to anyone reading CI logs. Truncate to a head + tail (e.g., `[hf_lit<...>yz]`) — enough for the engineer to identify which token shape triggered, not enough to use the secret.
